14 <script src="https://cookie-cdn.cookiepro.com/scripttemplates/otSDKStub.js" type="text/javascript" charset="UTF-8" data-domain-script="39c5a2de-fbb1-4ee4-82f8-a55723d5193e" class="cookiepro-otsdkstub"></script>

15 <script type="text/javascript">

16 function OptanonWrapper() {

17 // Get initial OnetrustActiveGroups ids

18 if (typeof OptanonWrapperCount == "undefined") {

19 otGetInitialGrps();

20 }

21

22 //Delete cookies

23 otDeleteCookie(otIniGrps);

24

25 // Assign OnetrustActiveGroups to custom variable

26 function otGetInitialGrps() {

27 OptanonWrapperCount = '';

28 otIniGrps = OnetrustActiveGroups;

29 }

30

31 function otDeleteCookie(iniOptGrpId) {

32 var otDomainGrps = JSON.parse(JSON.stringify(Optanon.GetDomainData().Groups));

33 var otDeletedGrpIds = otGetInactiveId(iniOptGrpId, OnetrustActiveGroups);

34 if (otDeletedGrpIds.length != 0 && otDomainGrps.length != 0) {

35 for (var i = 0; i < otDomainGrps.length; i++) {

36 //Check if CustomGroupId matches

37 if (otDomainGrps[i]['CustomGroupId'] != '' && otDeletedGrpIds.includes(otDomainGrps[i]['CustomGroupId'])) {

38 for (var j = 0; j < otDomainGrps[i]['Cookies'].length; j++) {

39 // console.log("otDeleteCookie",otDomainGrps[i]['Cookies'][j]['Name'])

40 //Delete cookie

41 eraseCookie(otDomainGrps[i]['Cookies'][j]['Name']);

42 }

43 }

44

45 //Check if Hostid matches

46 if (otDomainGrps[i]['Hosts'].length != 0) {

47 for (var j = 0; j < otDomainGrps[i]['Hosts'].length; j++) {

48 //Check if HostId presents in the deleted list and cookie array is not blank

49 if (otDeletedGrpIds.includes(otDomainGrps[i]['Hosts'][j]['HostId']) && otDomainGrps[i]['Hosts'][j]['Cookies'].length != 0) {

50 for (var k = 0; k < otDomainGrps[i]['Hosts'][j]['Cookies'].length; k++) {

51 //Delete cookie

52 eraseCookie(otDomainGrps[i]['Hosts'][j]['Cookies'][k]['Name']);

53 }

54 }

55 }

56 }

57

58 }

59 }

60 otGetInitialGrps(); //Reassign new group ids

61 }

62

63 //Get inactive ids

64 function otGetInactiveId(customIniId, otActiveGrp) {

65 //Initial OnetrustActiveGroups

66 // console.log("otGetInactiveId",customIniId)

67 customIniId = customIniId.split(",");

68 customIniId = customIniId.filter(Boolean);

69

70 //After action OnetrustActiveGroups

71 otActiveGrp = otActiveGrp.split(",");

72 otActiveGrp = otActiveGrp.filter(Boolean);

73

74 var result = [];

75 for (var i = 0; i < customIniId.length; i++) {

76 if (otActiveGrp.indexOf(customIniId[i]) <= -1) {

77 result.push(customIniId[i]);

78 }

79 }

80 return result;

81 }

82

83 //Delete cookie

84 function eraseCookie(name) {

85 //Delete root path cookies

86 domainName = window.location.hostname;

87 document.cookie = name + '=; Max-Age=-99999999; Path=/;Domain=' + domainName;

88 document.cookie = name + '=; Max-Age=-99999999; Path=/;';

89

90 //Delete LSO incase LSO being used, cna be commented out.

91 localStorage.removeItem(name);

92

93 //Check for the current path of the page

94 pathArray = window.location.pathname.split('/');

95 //Loop through path hierarchy and delete potential cookies at each path.

96 for (var i = 0; i < pathArray.length; i++) {

97 if (pathArray[i]) {

98 //Build the path string from the Path Array e.g /site/login

99 var currentPath = pathArray.slice(0, i + 1).join('/');

100 document.cookie = name + '=; Max-Age=-99999999; Path=' + currentPath + ';Domain=' + domainName;

101 document.cookie = name + '=; Max-Age=-99999999; Path=' + currentPath + ';';

102 //Maybe path has a trailing slash!

103 document.cookie = name + '=; Max-Age=-99999999; Path=' + currentPath + '/;Domain=' + domainName;

104 document.cookie = name + '=; Max-Age=-99999999; Path=' + currentPath + '/;';

105

106

107 }

108 }

109

110 }

111 }

2347 <div id="ContentTab" class="content active">

2356

2357

2358 <div id="8952961" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="0">

2359

2360 <section class="abstract"><p>We describe the discovery of an agonist of the nuclear receptor NR2F1 that specifically activates dormancy programs in malignant cells. The agonist led to a self-regulated increase in NR2F1 mRNA and protein and downstream transcription of a novel dormancy program. This program led to growth arrest of an HNSCC PDX line, human cell lines, and patient-derived organoids in 3D cultures and in vivo. This effect was lost when NR2F1 was knocked out by CRISPR-Cas9. RNA sequencing revealed that agonist treatment induces transcriptional changes associated with inhibition of cell cycle progression and mTOR signaling, metastasis suppression, and induction of a neural crest lineage program. In mice, agonist treatment resulted in inhibition of lung HNSCC metastasis, even after cessation of the treatment, where disseminated tumor cells displayed an NR2F1<sup>hi</sup>/p27<sup>hi</sup>/Ki-67<sup>lo</sup>/p-S6<sup>lo</sup> phenotype and remained in a dormant single-cell state. Our work provides proof of principle supporting the use of NR2F1 agonists to induce dormancy as a therapeutic strategy to prevent metastasis.</p></section>

2361 </div>

2362 <div class="article-metadata-panel clearfix rs_skip">

2363 <div class="widget-SolrResourceMetadata widget-instance-ContentMetadata_ArticleFulltext_Article">

2364

2366 <div class="content-metadata-subjects-title">Subjects: </div>

2367 <a class="solid-tumors" href="/jem/search-results?f_Subjects=Solid+tumors">Solid tumors</a>

2375 <h2 scrollto-destination="8952974" data-legacyid="s1" id="8952974" data-section-title="Introduction" class="section-title jumplink-heading">Introduction</h2>

2376 <div >

2377 <div id="8952975" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952974">

2378

2379 <p>Metastasis is the leading cause of cancer-related deaths and arises from disseminated tumor cells (DTCs) that seed secondary organs. These DTCs can remain dormant for years or decades before growing into symptomatic overt metastases (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib2" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Aguirre-Ghiso, 2007</a>). This period of latency occurs in multiple cancer types and is not limited to distant organs, as there can be loco-regional recurrences, a problem that is particularly significant in head and neck squamous cell carcinoma (HNSCC). More than half the patients with advanced disease develop recurrences that are usually not responsive to conventional therapies (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib41" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sacco and Cohen, 2015</a>). This highlights the need for interventional therapies that prevent reactivation of dormant DTCs after treatment of primary tumors, a window of treatment opportunity that is currently missed.</p>

2380 </div>

2381 <div id="8952976" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952974">

2382

2383 <p>We identified the nuclear receptor subfamily 2 group F member 1 (NR2F1; also known as COUP-TF1) as a master regulator of tumor cell dormancy (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). NR2F1 is an orphan nuclear receptor of the steroid/thyroid hormone receptors superfamily. It has a DNA-binding domain with two conserved zinc-finger motifs and a ligand-binding domain (LBD; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib51" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Tsai and Tsai, 1997</a>). It regulates transcription either directly by binding as a dimer to direct repeats on DNA and recruiting coactivator or corepressor complexes or indirectly by acting as a cofactor to other nuclear receptors (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib7" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bertacchi et al., 2019</a>). Depending on the context, NR2F1 can activate or repress transcription of effector genes (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib7" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bertacchi et al., 2019</a>). NR2F1 also plays an epigenetic role in mediating global histone modifications by interacting with or recruiting chromatin-remodeling enzymes (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib7" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bertacchi et al., 2019</a>).</p>

2384 </div>

2385 <div id="8952977" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952974">

2386

2387 <p>We found that NR2F1 is epigenetically silenced in proliferating cancer cells, and it is up-regulated in dormant residual HNSCC cells in a patient-derived xenograft (PDX) model and prostate cancer DTCs isolated from patients (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). In HNSCC patients, NR2F1 expression is absent or low in primary tumors, recurrent tumors, and metastases compared with benign adjacent oral mucosa (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). When up-regulated, NR2F1 induces expression of a dormancy gene signature, including the transcription factors SOX9 and retinoic acid (RA) receptor β (RARβ). These factors activate expression of cyclin-dependent kinase (CDK) inhibitors p27 and p16, which leads to G0/G1 cell cycle arrest and cell quiescence (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). NR2F1-associated signature is also enriched in estrogen receptor<sup>+</sup> breast cancer tumors, and patients carrying primary lesions enriched for this signature showed delayed time to metastasis (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib22" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Kim et al., 2012</a>). More specific to DTC biology, we also reported that breast cancer patients who carried in their bone marrow DTCs positive for NR2F1 were less likely to develop and die from bone metastasis compared with those negative or low for NR2F1 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib9" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Borgen et al., 2018</a>). NR2F1 is also associated with alterations in a breast cancer susceptibility locus (Mcs1; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib42" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Smits et al., 2013</a>). These data argue that NR2F1 is a strong negative regulator of HNSCC, breast cancer, and other cancers and that a unique function of NR2F1 is the induction of cancer cell dormancy, likely a role related to its lineage commitment function in development (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib39" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Rada-Iglesias et al., 2012</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib54" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Zhuang and Gudas, 2008</a>).</p>

2388 </div>

2389 <div id="8952978" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952974">

2390

2391 <p>shRNA-mediated down-regulation of NR2F1 results in reactivation of dormant HNSCC tumor cells, leading to locoregional relapse in surgery margins and development of lung and splenic metastasis in mice (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). This shows that NR2F1 plays a critical role in initiating and maintaining tumor cell dormancy, a biological function that has been reproduced by independent laboratories and ours in various cancer models (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib11" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Cackowski et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib22" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Kim et al., 2012</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib28" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Liu et al., 2018</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Epigenetic reprogramming therapy using low-dose 5-azacytidine (AZA; a DNMT1 inhibitor) and RA results in an up-regulation of NR2F1 and dormancy induction in various cancer models (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). While NR2F1 is silenced, it is not completely absent in many tumors (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib22" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Kim et al., 2012</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Additionally, although not directly tested, NR2F1 is predicted to regulate its own expression based on computational analysis and chromatin status data (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib1" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Adam et al., 2009</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Hence, experimental and clinical data support that activating NR2F1 using a small-molecule agonist represents an attractive clinical strategy to induce dormancy and prevent recurrence and metastasis by restoring its expression and function in DTCs. Here, we report the discovery of an NR2F1 selective agonist that was used as a proof-of-concept tool compound to induce and activate NR2F1 leading to the powerful activation of a dormancy program in cancer cells. Importantly, use of this agonist in a preclinical neoadjuvant and adjuvant setting completely suppresses macrometastatic growth by inducing DTC dormancy. This anti-metastatic effect can persist even after cessation of the treatment, arguing for a durable reprogramming of malignant cells into dormancy. Our work reveals a proof-of-principle strategy to limit metastatic growth by activation of dormancy mechanisms.</p>

2392 </div>

2393 </div>

2394 <h2 scrollto-destination="8952979" data-legacyid="s2" id="8952979" data-section-title="Results" class="section-title jumplink-heading">Results</h2>

2395 <div >

2396 </div>

2397 <h3 scrollto-destination="8952980" data-legacyid="s3" id="8952980" data-section-title="Modeling of the NR2F1 LBD identifies NR2F1 agonists in silico" class="section-title ">Modeling of the NR2F1 LBD identifies NR2F1 agonists in silico</h3>

2398 <div >

2399 <div id="8952981" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952980">

2400

2401 <p>There is no available crystal structure of NR2F1 or its LBD. We aimed to build a structural model based on homologous proteins of known structure, among which the most similar to NR2F1 is NR2F2 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib23" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Kruse et al., 2008</a>). However, the available NR2F2 structure is in the autorepressed conformation, in which the agonist-binding site is occluded. Hence, we built a model of NR2F1 LBD using as template the structure of RA receptor α (RXRα) in complex with 9-cis-RA (PDB accession no. 1FM6, ∼40% sequence identify to NR2F1 over the LBD; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib19" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Gampe et al., 2000</a>). The resulting NR2F1 LBD model represents its active conformation and was used in a structure-based virtual screening approach to identify small molecules with the potential to act as NR2F1 agonists (<a reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 1 A</a>).</p>

2402 </div>

2403 <div id="8952983" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952980">

2404

2405 <p>AutoDock and eHiTS programs were used to screen a library of 110,000 drug-like compounds. The top 50 compounds identified by each program (based on docking score) were combined with the top 50 compounds ranked by the sum of their AutoDock 4 and eHiTS ranks. All selected compounds were visually inspected in the context of the target-binding site to select the most promising ones. Of the 150 initial compounds, 67 were selected for experimental validation using a RA response element (RARE) luciferase reporter system in HEK293T cells with NR2F1 overexpression (data not shown). Of the 67 compounds, one agonist herein referred to as compound C26 (C26; chemical structure shown in <a reveal-id="figS1" data-open="figS1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S1 A</a>), consistently and significantly resulted in RARE reporter activation and was chosen for further validation. A model of NR2F1 in complex with C26, generated using the methods described above (<a reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 1 B</a>), shows that C26 interacts with mostly hydrophobic residues in the binding pocket (<a reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 1 C</a>). C26 was found to induce luciferase expression from RARE reporter by 2.4-fold, a level that is comparable to 100 nM all-trans retinoic acid (<em>at</em>RA; <a reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 1 D</a>), which activates transcription of target genes with RARE via binding to RARs (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib6" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Arisi et al., 2014</a>). Since the RARE-luciferase bioassay reports on the combined signaling of RARs and NR2F1 as a coactivator (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib26" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Lin et al., 2000</a>), we used another reporter system where luciferase expression is driven by NR2F1 cis-regulatory element in dormant HEp3 (D-HEp3) HNSCC cells that express high endogenous levels of NR2F1 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib1" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Adam et al., 2009</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Results show that C26 treatment significantly induces luciferase expression by 1.7- and 1.9-fold at 0.5 and 1 µM, respectively (<a reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 1 E</a>), further confirming that C26 acts as an NR2F1 agonist. To verify that the effect of C26 on reporter activation is specific, we generated D-HEp3 cell lines where NR2F1 is knocked out by CRISPR-Cas9 using two different guide RNAs (gRNAs), which was confirmed by Western blot (<a reveal-id="figS1" data-open="figS1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S1 B</a>). While C26 activates RARE- and NR2F1-luciferase reporters in control cells expressing nontargeting (NT) gRNA, the effect of C26 is completely abrogated in NR2F1 knockout cell lines (<a reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 1, F and G</a>). This indicates that the C26 effect in cells is dependent on NR2F1 and confirms the on-target effect of C26. We next assessed whether C26 has the ability to bind and activate RXRα, whose structure was used to model NR2F1 LBD, using a time-resolved fluorescence resonance energy transfer coactivator assay in vitro. Importantly, C26 does not activate RXRα even at concentrations that are 5- to 10-fold above those that we use in our assays (<a reveal-id="figS1" data-open="figS1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S1 C</a>). These results further support that C26 is a selective agonist for NR2F1 in human cancer cells.</p>

2406 </div>

2407 </div>

2408 <h3 scrollto-destination="8952985" data-legacyid="s4" id="8952985" data-section-title="C26 up-regulates NR2F1 and downstream target genes that regulate dormancy" class="section-title ">C26 up-regulates NR2F1 and downstream target genes that regulate dormancy</h3>

2409 <div >

2410 <div id="8952986" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952985">

2411

2412 <p>We previously showed that NR2F1 knockdown reduces repressive chromatin marks on its own promoter allowing an active chromatin state (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Additionally, computational identification of a transcription factor network active in dormant HNSCC cells revealed that NR2F1 is a central node, and it was predicted to regulate its own expression (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib1" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Adam et al., 2009</a>). These observations suggest that upon its activation, NR2F1 might up-regulate its own expression and that even NR2F1<sup>lo</sup> tumor cells could be activated to reexpress this nuclear receptor. This has not been tested before due to lack of experimental tools to activate NR2F1. To test this, we used tumorigenic HEp3 (T-HEp3) cells, a highly proliferative HNSCC PDX line that was obtained from a lymph node metastasis with primary carcinoma in the buccal mucosa (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib33" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Moore et al., 1955</a>) and maintained in vivo. These cells express low but detectable levels of NR2F1 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). T-HEp3 cells were pretreated with DMSO or C26 and inoculated in vivo on chicken embryo chorioallantoic membrane (CAM) with continuous daily treatments. After 7 d, tumors were excised, and mRNA was extracted. Quantitative PCR (qPCR) analysis revealed that C26 treatment results in 2.3-fold increase in NR2F1 mRNA levels in tumors (<a reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 2 A</a>). To evaluate if C26 treatment also leads to up-regulation of NR2F1 protein, we performed a similar experiment, but this time, tumors were dissociated, and cytospins of cells were immunostained for NR2F1. T-HEp3 cells are distinguished from avian cells by immunostaining for vimentin, an intermediate filament that is abundantly expressed by HEp3 cells and has been previously used to detect these cells (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib10" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bragado et al., 2013</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). The percentage of NR2F1<sup>+</sup> cells is dramatically increased from ∼4% in DMSO control tumors, which is consistent with what was previously reported (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>), to 42% in tumors treated with C26 (<a reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 2, B and C</a>). This was determined using a stringent mask for strong nuclear NR2F1 signal, which appears as prominent clusters that we had reported previously in HNSCC and breast cancer DTCs (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib9" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Borgen et al., 2018</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). To further confirm the effect of C26 on NR2F1 expression, we used an in vitro 3D assay, where T-HEp3 cells were plated at low density in Matrigel and treated with DMSO or C26 for 4 d. Immunostaining for NR2F1 showed a significant increase in the mean fluorescence intensity (MFI) of nuclear NR2F1 in C26-treated cells compared with control (<a reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 2, D and E</a>). These data confirm that NR2F1 activation by C26 treatment results in up-regulation of NR2F1 mRNA and nuclear protein levels.</p>

2413 </div>

2414 <div id="8952988" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952985">

2415

2416 <p>NR2F1 induces tumor cell quiescence by binding to the promoters of SOX9, RARβ, and p27 and inducing their expression (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). To determine if activation of NR2F1 by C26 and its nuclear accumulation is accompanied by an increase in NR2F1 target gene expression, we measured the effect of C26 treatment on mRNA levels of these factors in CAM tumors. mRNA levels of SOX9, RARβ, and p27 are all significantly up-regulated in C26-treated tumors compared with control (<a reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 2 F</a>). Interestingly, C26 treatment has no effect on mRNA levels of DEC2 (<a reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 2 F</a>), a transcription factor that in dormant HNSCC and breast cancer cells is primarily regulated by TGF-β2 signaling (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib10" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bragado et al., 2013</a>). Hence, C26 does not affect the TGF-β2–DEC2 pathway, which has been shown to mediate dormancy signals in a manner that is parallel to and independent from NR2F1 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Collectively, these results show that C26 treatment induces NR2F1 expression and nuclear accumulation and selectively activates canonical NR2F1-driven dormancy pathway genes.</p>

2417 </div>

2418 </div>

2419 <h3 scrollto-destination="8952989" data-legacyid="s5" id="8952989" data-section-title="RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) analysis reveals that NR2F1 activation induces neural crest–like and growth suppression programs in HNSCC cells" class="section-title ">RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) analysis reveals that NR2F1 activation induces neural crest–like and growth suppression programs in HNSCC cells</h3>

2420 <div >

2421 <div id="8952990" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952989">

2422

2423 <p>To gain an unbiased insight on transcriptional changes that are induced by C26-mediated activation of NR2F1, we performed RNA-seq of T-HEp3 tumors treated with C26 in vivo. T-HEp3-GFP cells were pretreated in culture with C26 or DMSO for 6 d and then inoculated on CAM with continuous daily treatment. After 7 d, tumors were recovered and dissociated, GFP<sup>+</sup> cells were isolated using FACS (<a reveal-id="figS2" data-open="figS2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S2 A</a>), and mRNA was sequenced using next-generation sequencing. Principal-component analysis (PCA) of the RNA-seq data using the top 500 genes with the highest variation in gene expression across samples revealed that replicate samples from each condition cluster together and away from the other condition (<a reveal-id="figS2" data-open="figS2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S2 B</a>). Bioinformatics analysis showed that 1,324 genes were down-regulated and 456 genes were up-regulated by C26 treatment compared with DMSO control (<a reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 3 A</a>). Gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA) showed that the Hallmark gene sets with the highest normalized enrichment score in DMSO control (hence negatively regulated by C26 treatment) included epithelial-to-mesenchymal transition (EMT), down-regulation of UV light response, mTORC1 pathway, protein secretion, and oxidative phosphorylation (<a reveal-id="figS2" data-open="figS2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S2 C</a>). EMT is also one of the most significantly down-regulated pathways in C26 in the WikiPathways database (<a reveal-id="figS2" data-open="figS2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S2 D</a>; left panel). Interestingly, C26 treatment also significantly down-regulates the phosphoinositide 3-kinase (PI3K) signaling pathway, which is activated in more than 90% of HNSCC cases and plays a critical role in the pathogenesis of HNSCC (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib32" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Marquard and Jücker, 2020</a>). This is evidenced by the focal adhesion_PI3K_AKT_mTOR and the PI3K_AKT signaling pathways being the two most significantly down-regulated pathways from the WikiPathways database in C26-treated samples (<a reveal-id="figS2" data-open="figS2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S2 D</a>; left panel) and the significant enrichment of the Hallmark PI3K_AKT_mTOR pathway in DMSO control (<a reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 3 B</a>). Since NR2F1 is known to regulate neural crest gene expression during development (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib39" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Rada-Iglesias et al., 2012</a>), we explored if the neural crest differentiation pathway was up-regulated upon C26 treatment. Results show that this pathway is significantly enriched in C26-treated samples (<a reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 3 C</a>) and is among the top up-regulated pathways from the WikiPathways database (<a reveal-id="figS2" data-open="figS2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S2 D</a>; right panel). In agreement, 27 genes involved in cranial neural crest cell development (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib8" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Betancur et al., 2010</a>) are significantly up-regulated upon C26 treatment (<a reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 3 D</a>). Among these, C26 treatment up-regulated NOTCH1, which is found mutated in a fraction of HNSCC tumors and is thought to restrict progression (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib49" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Stransky et al., 2011</a>), and ZIC1, a negative regulator of sonic hedgehog signaling that is also commonly methylated in HNSCC (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib36" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Paluszczak et al., 2017</a>).</p>

2424 </div>

2425 <div id="8952993" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952989">

2426

2427 <p>To further narrow the effect of C26 on transcriptional changes, we performed a pathway enrichment analysis using Ingenuity Pathway Analysis (IPA; <a reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 3 E</a>), which revealed that transcriptional changes induced by C26 are predicted to cause inhibition of cell cycle progression and proliferation as well as suppression of invasion and metastasis. Interestingly, one of the signaling nodes that are inhibited upon C26 treatment is TGF-β1, which we have previously shown to induce reactivation of dormant HNSCC cells (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib10" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bragado et al., 2013</a>). IPA analysis also revealed Sox1, Sox3, and GMNN, which are transcription factors linked to neural crest stem cell lineage function, as significant activity nodes. While the function of these factors remains unknown in the context of NR2F1 activation, they are involved in one or more processes that regulate neural crest cell development and differentiation and stem cell fate decisions (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib15" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Ekonomou et al., 2005</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib21" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Kan et al., 2007</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib40" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Rizzoti et al., 2004</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib45" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Spella et al., 2011</a>). We conclude that C26 induces a program of growth and metastasis suppression associated with a reduction in the PI3K–mTOR pathway and induction of components of a developmental program found in differentiating neural crest cells.</p>

2428 </div>

2429 <div id="8952994" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952989">

2430

2431 <p>We next aimed to determine to what extent the transcriptional changes that are induced in C26 treatment are found in spontaneously dormant HNSCC cells, as described previously (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). We compared RNA-seq data from our current C26 treatment study with RNA-seq data comparing dormant D-HEp3 cells to proliferative T-HEp3 cells. This analysis showed that the genes regulated by C26 activation of NR2F1 represent only a fraction of the genes regulated in D-HEp3 cells. Of these, 446 genes that are down-regulated by C26 treatment are also down-regulated in dormant cells, and 108 genes that are up-regulated by C26 treatment are also up-regulated in dormant cells (<a reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 3 F</a>). Comparing GSEA of both datasets revealed that a subset of the Hallmark pathways that are significantly down-regulated in C26, including the EMT signature, are also down-regulated in spontaneously dormant cells (<a class="supplementary-material-link" reveal-id="all-supplement-materials-popup" data-open="all-supplement-materials-popup" path-from-xml="sup1">Table S1</a>; left column). However, there were differences between these programs as well (<a class="supplementary-material-link" reveal-id="all-supplement-materials-popup" data-open="all-supplement-materials-popup" path-from-xml="sup1">Table S1</a>; right column). For example, the mTORC1 signaling pathway is significantly down-regulated after C26 treatment, but not in D-HEp3 cells. Of note, although far less statistically significant, a subset of genes was modulated in the opposite direction in D-HEp3 cells (i.e., down-regulated in D-HEp3/up-regulated in C26 and up-regulated in D-HEp3/down-regulated in C26; <a reveal-id="figS2" data-open="figS2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S2 E</a>). We conclude that C26-mediated activation of NR2F1 in malignant cells controls a subprogram of gene expression found in spontaneously dormant D-HEp3 cells but that C26 also modulates a distinct subset of unique gene expression programs.</p>

2432 </div>

2433 <div id="8952995" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952989">

2434

2435 <p>Finally, to corroborate the RNA-seq gene expression findings and assess the specificity of the C26 effect on gene expression, we used CRISPR-Cas9 to knock out NR2F1 in T-HEp3 cells and confirmed the knockout by Western blot (<a reveal-id="figS3" data-open="figS3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S3 A</a>). We then performed qPCR analysis of multiple markers from the neural crest cell development pathway (neurogenin 1, cadherin 6, SOX10, and PHOX2B), as well as NR2F1 downstream effectors (p27, RARβ, and SOX9, which is also part of the neural crest cell development pathway). The results show that C26 treatment significantly increases expression of neurogenin 1, cadherin 6, SOX10, and PHOX2B (<a reveal-id="figS3" data-open="figS3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S3 B</a>; left panel), which validates the RNA-seq results. Importantly, the effect of C26 on gene expression of these markers, as well as p27, RAR, and SOX9, was completely abrogated in the R2F1 knockout cell line (<a reveal-id="figS3" data-open="figS3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S3 B</a>; right panel), indicating that this effect is specifically mediated by NR2F1.</p>

2436 </div>

2437 </div>

2438 <h3 scrollto-destination="8952997" data-legacyid="s6" id="8952997" data-section-title="C26 induces growth arrest via NR2F1" class="section-title ">C26 induces growth arrest via NR2F1</h3>

2439 <div >

2440 <div id="8952998" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952997">

2441

2442 <p>Our RNA-seq data showing that C26 treatment induces transcriptional changes associated with growth suppression and lineage commitment, and the fact that we had shown that NR2F1 controls a cancer cell quiescence program in HNSCC and other cancer cells (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>), led us to test its effect on malignant tumor cell growth. To this end, results showed that C26 treatment of T-HEp3 cells plated in Matrigel kept them in a single-cell stage, as evidenced by the higher percentage of single cells (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4 A</a>) and lower percentage of clusters (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4 B</a>) compared with DMSO. Similar results were obtained in two separate HNSCC cell lines, FaDu and SQ20B (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4 C</a>). Accordingly, staining for the proliferation marker Ki-67 in the Matrigel assay showed that C26 significantly decreased the percentage of Ki-67<sup>+</sup> cells in T-HEp3, SQ20B, and FaDu cells compared with DMSO (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Figs. 4 D</a> and <a reveal-id="figS4" data-open="figS4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">S4 A</a>). These data collectively indicate that C26 treatments arrest HNSCC cells in a nonproliferative single-cell state. We then evaluated the effect of C26 on tumor growth in vivo in the CAM model. T-HEp3 cells were pretreated for 6 d in culture and then inoculated on CAM, with or without continuous treatment. Results show that C26 inhibits tumor growth on CAM both with pretreatment only (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4 E</a>) and, to a higher extent, with continuous treatment (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4 F</a>). Staining for cleaved caspase-3 revealed that there is no difference in the percentage of cells that are positive for this apoptotic marker between control and treated groups, which indicates that C26 treatment does not induce apoptotic cell death (<a reveal-id="figS4" data-open="figS4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S4 B</a>).</p>

2443 </div>

2444 <div id="8953001" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952997">

2445

2446 <p>To assess if C26 treatment has an effect on tumor cells directly derived from patients with HNSCC, we established a patient-derived organoid line following previously established methods (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib14" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Driehuis et al., 2019</a>). After establishing the propagatable organoid line, organoids were dissociated into single cells and plated in Matrigel with DMSO or C26. By determining the percentage of single cells and colonies after 7 d of culture, we found that C26 significantly inhibits the organoid formation efficiency, where cells are arrested in a single-cell state (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4, G and H</a>). Additionally, organoids that form have a markedly smaller area in C26 treatment compared with DMSO (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4 I</a>). These results indicate that C26 treatment has the ability to induce quiescence of cells directly derived from patient tumors.</p>

2447 </div>

2448 <div id="8953002" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952997">

2449

2450 <p>Our RNA-seq and growth arrest data suggest that C26-mediated activation of NR2F1 induces cell cycle arrest. To further support this conclusion, we used T-HEp3 cells expressing a previously described DNA Helicase B (DHB)-Venus, a CDK2 biosensor that translocates from the nucleus to the cytoplasm upon phosphorylation by CDK2 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib12" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Di Martino et al., 2021</a>,<em>Preprint</em>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib46" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Spencer et al., 2013</a>). Hence, this biosensor enables the identification of the different phases of the cell cycle using fluorescent microscopy, where cells in G0/G1 phase show nuclear accumulation, cells in S phase show both nuclear and cytoplasmic localization, and cells in G2 phase show cytoplasmic and no nuclear localization (<a reveal-id="figS4" data-open="figS4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S4 C</a>). Cells were pretreated with C26 for 48 h, plated on Matrigel, and treated for an additional 48 h. Cells were then fixed, stained with DAPI, and imaged. Using this approach, we show that C26 treatment significantly increases the percentage of cells in G0/G1 from 7% to 41% while decreasing the percentage of cells in S and G2/M phases (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4 J</a>). These results indicate that NR2F1 activation by C26 induces cell cycle arrest at G0/G1.</p>

2451 </div>

2452 <div id="8953003" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8952997">

2453

2454 <p>To confirm that the effect of C26 on cell growth is mediated by NR2F1, we used T-HEp3 cell lines in which NR2F1 is knocked out by CRISPR-Cas9 using two different gRNAs (g2 and g4 in <a reveal-id="figS3" data-open="figS3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S3 A</a>). C26 effect on these cells lines was evaluated in the previously described 3D Matrigel assay. While C26 increases the percentage of single cells and decreases the number of clusters in cells with NT gRNA in a manner similar to the parental cell line, the effect of C26 was completely abrogated in the two NR2F1 knockout cell lines (<a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4, K and L</a>). However, knockdown of NR2F2 using two different siRNAs (see <a reveal-id="figS4" data-open="figS4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S4 D</a> for qPCR verification) did not alter the effect of C26. C26 treatment of cells with NR2F2 knockdown increased the percentage of single cells (<a reveal-id="figS4" data-open="figS4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S4 E</a>) and decreased the percentage of clusters (<a reveal-id="figS4" data-open="figS4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S4 F</a>) compared with DMSO similar to cells with control siRNA (compare to <a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4, A, B, K, and L</a>). These results, along with the in vivo detection of dormancy genes detected in <a reveal-id="fig2" data-open="fig2" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 2 F</a> and the selectivity controls showed in <a reveal-id="fig1" data-open="fig1" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 1</a>, indicate that the growth-suppressive effects of C26 in cancer cells is tightly dependent on an NR2F1-driven dormancy program.</p>

2455 </div>

2456 </div>

2457 <h3 scrollto-destination="8953004" data-legacyid="s7" id="8953004" data-section-title="C26 inhibits primary tumor growth and metastatic growth in lungs" class="section-title ">C26 inhibits primary tumor growth and metastatic growth in lungs</h3>

2458 <div >

2459 <div id="8953005" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953004">

2460

2461 <p>The above RNA-seq data and growth experiments establish that C26 activates NR2F1 and induces dormancy of HNSCC cells in in vitro 3D models and in vivo on CAM. However, the CAM system does not allow monitoring long-term phenotypes in target organs, since tumors can be grown for a maximum of 7 d. Thus, we assessed whether C26-mediated activation of NR2F1 induces tumor cell dormancy and inhibits metastatic growth in mice. GFP-tagged T-HEp3 PDX cells were injected subcutaneously in BALB/c nu/nu mice. Tumors were allowed to develop and reach 300 mm<sup>3</sup> in volume, after which neoadjuvant treatment was administered i.p. for 5 d. After 2 d of rest, tumors were surgically removed, and four cycles of adjuvant treatment (5 d of treatment and 2 d of rest) were administered (<a reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 5 A</a>). Excised tumors were weighed at the time of surgery, and results unexpectedly showed that C26 treatment inhibited primary tumor growth in 8 out 12 mice (<a reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 5 B</a>). Analysis of resected tumors for NR2F1 expression showed that as early as 1 wk after neoadjuvant treatment, C26 induced up-regulation of NR2F1 expression in primary tumors (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5 A</a>). This data supports that C26 dosed in mice with the current schedule engages the target and activates NR2F1 function and expression. Importantly, C26 treatment did not induce apoptosis of proliferating primary tumor cells as evident by the absence of change in percentage of cleaved caspase-3<sup>+</sup> cells compared with DMSO (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5 B</a>).</p>

2462 </div>

2463 <div id="8953008" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953004">

2464

2465 <p>Lungs were collected when the mice were sacrificed at the end of the adjuvant treatment period. One lung lobe was processed for collagenase dissociation, while other lobes were prepared for formalin fixation and paraffin embedding. Analysis of the total number of GFP<sup>+</sup> tumor cells per lung lobe revealed that mice treated with C26 had almost half the number of tumor cells compared with control mice (<a reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 5 C</a>). This metastasis inhibitory effect of C26 is independent of the effect on primary tumor growth, since primary tumor weight and number of lung DTCs are not correlated (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5 C</a>). Since the above analysis does not distinguish between solitary DTCs and large or small metastatic lesion frequency, we immunostained lungs using an antibody for vimentin, which has been previously used to detect T-HEp3 cells in lungs (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib10" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bragado et al., 2013</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>), and determined the number of mice with solitary cells only, micrometastases (&lt;50 cells), and macrometastases (&gt;50 cells; <a reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 5 D</a>). Results showed that 33% and 50% of DMSO-treated mice have micrometastases only or micrometastases and macrometastases in their lungs, respectively. However, 0% of mice treated with C26 showed micro- or macrometastases, and only single DTCs persisted in the lungs of these mice (<a reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 5 E</a>). We have previously reported that in this aggressive model of HNSCC and in the absence of NR2F1, solitary DTCs in lungs transition out of a short-term quiescence and form metastasis (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Our current data indicate that while tumor cells were able to disseminate to the lungs and lodge in a solitary cell state as we showed previously (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib10" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bragado et al., 2013</a>), C26 treatment prevents single cells from dividing and growing into overt metastases. This is consistent with what we found in vitro in the 3D Matrigel cultures, where C26 treatment arrested cells in a single-cell state.</p>

2466 </div>

2467 <div id="8953009" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953004">

2468

2469 <p>In a parallel group to the two groups presented above, we combined in the neoadjuvant phase a pretreatment with AZA, <em>at</em>RA, and C26 (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5 D</a>). This treatment shortened the time of C26 treatment to accommodate the combination. The rationale for this treatment is that low NR2F1 expression in T-HEp3 cells may not allow for a full effect of the C26 agonist. As shown previously, the AZA+<em>at</em>RA treatment may restore NR2F1 expression (among other gene expression changes) and allow for more receptor to be available for the C26 treatment. However, the AZA+<em>at</em>RA pretreatment did not improve the effect of C26 on primary tumor or metastatic growth (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5, E and F</a>). In the primary tumors the lack of effect could be due to the shorter exposure to C26 or an interfering effect from the AZA+<em>at</em>RA treatment. Nevertheless, the results show that C26 as a single agent can engage the target systemically and suppress metastasis development.</p>

2470 </div>

2471 <div id="8953010" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953004">

2472

2473 <p>To assess the reversibility and stability of the metastasis suppression phenotype caused by C26 treatment, we performed an experimental metastasis experiment by injecting T-HEp3 cells into the tail vein of BALB/c nu/nu mice. These cells were treated in culture with DMSO or C26 for 1 wk before injection similar to <a reveal-id="fig4" data-open="fig4" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 4, E and F</a>. Mice injected with DMSO-pretreated cells were treated with DMSO in vehicle for 3 wk via i.p. delivery. Mice injected with C26 pretreated cells were divided into two groups. The first group was treated continuously with C26 for 3 wk, and the second group was treated with C26 for 1 wk, then the treatment was stopped and followed by 2 wk of DMSO. At the end of treatment, mice were euthanized, and the resected lungs were stained with H&amp;E and analyzed for metastasis frequency and burden. Our results show that significantly, both continuous and interrupted treatments with C26 inhibited metastasis to the same extent as evidenced by a comparable decrease in number of metastases (frequency) as well as the percentage of lung area covered with metastases (burden) compared with DMSO (<a reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 5, F–H</a>).</p>

2474 </div>

2475 <div id="8953011" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953004">

2476

2477 <p>To determine if C26 treatment causes adverse toxic effects in mice, we monitored the weight of the mice weekly, and at the end of the treatment, we performed autopsy, complete blood count, and liver and kidney function analysis. Neither continuous nor interrupted C26 treatment induced weight loss (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5 G</a>). Additionally, as determined by a veterinary pathologist, there were no signs of gross, blood, kidney, or liver toxicity upon C26 treatment. These results indicated that at least for 3 wk, C26 induces a potent and, as predicted, self-sustained long-lasting metastasis inhibitory effect, with no evidence of toxicity.</p>

2478 </div>

2479 </div>

2480 <h3 scrollto-destination="8953012" data-legacyid="s8" id="8953012" data-section-title="C26 suppresses lung metastasis by inducing an NR2F1&lt;sup&gt;hi&lt;/sup&gt;/p27&lt;sup&gt;hi&lt;/sup&gt;/Ki67&lt;sup&gt;lo&lt;/sup&gt;/p-S6&lt;sup&gt;lo&lt;/sup&gt; dormancy profile in solitary DTCs" class="section-title ">C26 suppresses lung metastasis by inducing an NR2F1<sup>hi</sup>/p27<sup>hi</sup>/Ki67<sup>lo</sup>/p-S6<sup>lo</sup> dormancy profile in solitary DTCs</h3>

2481 <div >

2482 <div id="8953013" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953012">

2483

2484 <p>We next sought to determine if the inability of lung DTCs to proliferate and grow into overt metastases in C26-treated mice is due to induction of NR2F1 and a dormancy phenotype. To verify that single DTCs in C26-treated mice are nonproliferative dormant cells, we immunostained lungs, which were collected from the previously described spontaneous dissemination experiment (<a reveal-id="fig5" data-open="fig5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 5 A</a>), for Ki-67 and determined the percentage of proliferative Ki-67<sup>+</sup>/vimentin<sup>+</sup> tumor cells in lungs. While 45% of human-specific vimentin<sup>+</sup> tumor cells in control are Ki-67<sup>+</sup>, this is significantly reduced to 7% in C26-treated mice (<a reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 6, A and B</a>). Importantly, more than 99% tumor cells in the lungs of both DMSO and C26-treated mice were negative for cleaved caspase-3 (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5, H and I</a>), indicating that solitary DTCs in C26-treated lungs are growth arrested, but not apoptotic. These data show that single DTCs in lungs of C26-treated mice are in a quiescent nonproliferative state. To determine if these single DTCs are in a dormant state, we immunostained for NR2F1, which not only is the target of C26, but also serves as a dormancy marker and is more informative on time to metastasis than proliferation markers alone, as we showed in breast cancer DTCs from patients (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib9" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Borgen et al., 2018</a>). Results showed that lung DTCs in C26-treated had significantly higher NR2F1 expression, as evidenced by an increase in the percentage of NR2F1<sup>hi</sup> cells (<a reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 6, A and C</a>) and MFI per nucleus (<a reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 6, A and D</a>). Interestingly, NR2F1 expression was significantly higher in DTCs of C26-treated mice compared with solitary DTCs in DMSO-treated mice (<a reveal-id="figS5" data-open="figS5" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. S5 J</a>). We also performed a similar analysis for the CDK inhibitor p27, which is a target of NR2F1 and also serves as a complementary dormancy marker (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib43" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2014</a>). The percentage of cells with nuclear accumulation of p27 (<a reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 6, E and F</a>) and nuclear p27 MFI (<a reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 6, E and G</a>) are significantly higher in lung DTCs of C26-treated mice (<a reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 6, E and F</a>). Altogether, these results confirm that C26 treatment prevents outgrowth of lung metastases by activating NR2F1, which up-regulates its own expression as well as downstream targets, including p27, and keeps DTCs in a dormant state.</p>

2485 </div>

2486 <div id="8953015" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953012">

2487

2488 <p>Our RNA-seq analysis revealed a suppression of mTOR signaling pathway upon C26 treatment (<a reveal-id="fig3" data-open="fig3" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 3 B</a>). This was a surprising finding, as we had not previously connected NR2F1 signaling to mTOR signaling. To assess if this pathway is down-regulated in solitary DTCs in C26-treated mice, we immunostained for phosphorylated ribosomal protein S6 (p-S6), which is a downstream readout for mTOR pathway activation (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib27" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Liu and Sabatini, 2020</a>). This analysis showed that while ∼50% of tumor cells in lungs of DMSO-treated mice are positive for p-S6, none of the solitary DTCs in lungs of C26-treated mice were positive for this marker (<a reveal-id="fig6" data-open="fig6" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 6, H and I</a>). These data corroborate the findings from the RNA-seq analysis and further indicate that C26 treatment and NR2F1 activation function as novel suppressors of mTOR signaling coupled with a gain of neural crest differentiation markers in HNSCC cells.</p>

2489 </div>

2490 </div>

2491 <h3 scrollto-destination="8953016" data-legacyid="s9" id="8953016" data-section-title="NR2F1 levels and activation inform on solitary human DTCs state and disease progression in HNSCC" class="section-title ">NR2F1 levels and activation inform on solitary human DTCs state and disease progression in HNSCC</h3>

2492 <div >

2493 <div id="8953017" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953016">

2494

2495 <p>We previously reported that NR2F1 expression is silenced in primary tumors and overt HNSCC lymph node metastasis (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). However, we did not have access to lymph nodes with solitary residual DTCs to investigate whether NR2F1 is up-regulated when DTCs are in a solitary state and presumably dormant (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib48" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sproll et al., 2018</a>). To address this specific question, we stained patient samples for NR2F1 in normal adjacent epithelium, primary tumors, and lymph nodes that were confirmed to have solitary HNSCC DTCs, but not overt metastasis (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib48" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sproll et al., 2018</a>). Pan-cytokeratin (pan-CK) was used as a marker to identify cells of epithelial origin in all tissues (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib47" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sproll et al., 2017</a>). Quantitative image analysis was used to score pan-CK<sup>+</sup> DTCs as NR2F1<sup>hi</sup>, NR2F1<sup>med</sup>, or NR2F1<sup>−/low</sup>. This analysis revealed that the percentage of NR2F1<sup>hi</sup> cells drops from 28% in normal epithelium to 2.4% in primary tumors, while 20% of solitary DTCs are NR2F1<sup>hi</sup>. Conversely, the percentage of NR2F1<sup>−/low</sup> is 39% in normal epithelium, 84% in primary tumors, and 63% in solitary DTCs (<a reveal-id="fig7" data-open="fig7" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 7, A and B</a>). These results indicate that a subpopulation of solitary DTCs are more frequently positive for NR2F1 than cells in primary tumors, supporting that NR2F1 is dynamically regulated in cancer cells in primary versus secondary organs and could also mark dormant solitary DTCs in HNSCC patients.</p>

2496 </div>

2497 <div id="8953019" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953016">

2498

2499 <p>To further explore the relevance of the transcriptional changes induced by C26 treatment, we explored how the C26-induced dormancy signature in T-HEp3 cells is associated with outcomes of HNSCC patients. To this end, we generated a C26 gene signature (<a class="supplementary-material-link" reveal-id="all-supplement-materials-popup" data-open="all-supplement-materials-popup" path-from-xml="sup2">Table S2</a>) and assessed whether the presence of this signature in primary tumors from HNSCC patients revealed different patterns of overall survival or relapse-free survival using Kaplan–Meier plot analysis. The C26 gene signature includes the top 25 up-regulated and top 25 down-regulated genes that had the largest fold changes in the RNA-seq data we obtained, including neural crest cell differentiation genes (<a class="supplementary-material-link" reveal-id="all-supplement-materials-popup" data-open="all-supplement-materials-popup" path-from-xml="sup2">Table S2</a>). We also included NR2F1, as we had prior data showing that under certain conditions, such as hypoxia, it can be turned on in HNSCC primary tumors and spawn dormant DTCs (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>). Kaplan–Meier plots showed that the probability of both overall survival and relapse-free survival is significantly higher in patients carrying tumors with the C26 gene signature compared with patients without the signature (<a reveal-id="fig7" data-open="fig7" class="link link-reveal link-table xref-fig">Fig. 7 C</a>). These data support that a subpopulation of HNSCC patients carry primary tumors enriched for a unique NR2F1 activation dormancy signature that occurs spontaneously. Importantly, patients carrying these tumors had improved relapse-free survival, suggesting that cancer cells that disseminated locoregionally or distantly from these tumors may have taken longer to produce recurrences due to higher NR2F1 activity. Since the signature has no enrichment in proliferation versus growth arrest cell cycle genes, it may not be simply interpreted as a difference in proliferative potential in patients’ tumors.</p>

2500 </div>

2501 </div>

2502 <h2 scrollto-destination="8953020" data-legacyid="s10" id="8953020" data-section-title="Discussion" class="section-title jumplink-heading">Discussion</h2>

2503 <div >

2504 <div id="8953021" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953020">

2505

2506 <p>We previously discovered that NR2F1 is an important regulator of cancer cell dormancy (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>), a finding that was independently confirmed (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib11" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Cackowski et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib22" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Kim et al., 2012</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib28" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Liu et al., 2018</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). We sought to determine if this dormancy marker could be drugged to take advantage of its stable quiescence-inducing function. Our results using a structure-based in silico screening approach leveraged a new agonist (C26) that could accomplish our objective of turning “on” an NR2F1 pathway in malignant cancer cells. The inability of C26 to activate RXRα as well as the NR2F1 knockout and NR2F2 knockdown controls support the selectivity of C26. It is possible that a crystal structure of NR2F1 LBD, which as of now has not been solved yet, may yield more potent and selective agonists. NR2F2 also does not appear to be a target of C26 as the sgRNAs were designed to target only NR2F1, and KO of NR2F1, but not NR2F2 knockdown, eliminated the C26 effect. A significant finding was that C26 treatment resulted in autoinduction of NR2F1 expression in HNSCC T-HEp3 cells, which show silencing (albeit not complete) of NR2F1 through repressive histone modifications (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). Induction of endogenous NR2F1 by C26 suggests that this silencing is not as tight as previously expected and that, although not tested directly, engaging NR2F1 with C26 may allow it to rapidly remodel the repressive chromatin at promoters, turn on its own expression, and drive downstream signals for dormancy onset. The lack of additional effect of the AZA+<em>at</em>RA treatment on metastasis further supports that C26 can alone restore high NR2F1 expression and activity. Whether other epigenetic drugs such as histone deacetylase or bromodomain inhibitors (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib31" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Manzotti et al., 2019</a>) might have additive or synergistic effects with C26 is unknown and will be tested in future studies. This is interesting, as activating this orphan nuclear receptor may be useful in treating not only tumors with abundant expression of NR2F1 but also those that express low levels of NR2F1. NR2F1 agonists might be particularly useful in breast cancer, where NR2F1 expression is highly enriched in estrogen receptor<sup>+</sup> tumors. Interestingly, these are the patients that commonly show late relapse (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib22" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Kim et al., 2012</a>). Thus, agonists for NR2F1 may be used as a therapeutic approach to suppress reawakening of dormant cells kept in that state by anti-estrogen therapies or even in triple-negative and other breast cancer subtypes.</p>

2507 </div>

2508 <div id="8953022" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953020">

2509

2510 <p>We also wondered whether in vivo treatment with an NR2F1 agonist would be sufficient to prevent awakening of DTCs, and if the effect is long-lived. Our in vivo experiments in the CAM avian PDX system and in mice support that C26-mediated activation of NR2F1 induces a strong and long-lived growth suppressive signal that can persist even if the treatment is removed for at least 2 wk (equivalent to ∼1.6 yr in humans). While pretreatment of T-HEp3 cells in culture with C26 did not significantly inhibit cell proliferation in 2D culture in vitro (data not shown), it did suppress growth in 3D cultures, which was corroborated using a CDK2-biosensor of cell cycle progression, and 1 wk later in vivo on CAM, an effect that was stronger with continuous treatment in vivo. These data support that perhaps a treatment schedule that activates NR2F1 might not need to be continuous, as it may operate as a growth arrest program that is self-sustained, similar to what we previously reported for AZA and <em>at</em>RA (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>). A unique set of findings was that the C26-regulated program might be linked to a differentiation-like program in the neural crest lineage that may stabilize the growth arrest phenotype induced by C26 in DTCs. This program may be reinforced by the strong inhibition of S6-kinase, a canonical mTOR target. In the mouse PDX experiments, the neoadjuvant plus adjuvant strategy was chosen based on prior data showing that the very aggressive T-HEp3 PDX model spreads soon after subcutaneous implantation and that metastases can grow almost simultaneously with the primary tumor (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib10" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Bragado et al., 2013</a>). Nevertheless, our results strongly support that C26 was able to hold these DTCs, which are highly efficient at initiating metastasis (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib5" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Aguirre-Ghiso et al., 2004</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib35" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Ossowski et al., 1987</a>), in a dormant state. This result, and the stability of the C26 effect without any detectable toxicity, was further corroborated in the experimental metastasis assay that as performed here exclusively targeted freshly seeded DTCs in lungs. The dormancy phenotype caused by C26 was supported by the NR2F1<sup>hi</sup>/p27<sup>hi</sup>/Ki67<sup>lo</sup>/p-S6<sup>lo</sup> profile that these DTCs displayed. Furthermore, none of the animals treated with C26 displayed micro- or macrometastasis, strengthening the significance of activating NR2F1 to prevent metastatic relapse. Even when analyzing only NR2F1 levels specifically in solitary DTCs, which are more frequently spontaneously high for NR2F1 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>), we found that C26-treated animals displayed DTCs with higher NR2F1 expression than control animals. This corroborates that C26 might be preventing metastasis initiation by maintaining a dormant solitary cancer cell state. Surprisingly, C26 inhibited growth of some primary tumors and engaged the target in all tested tumors regardless of size. This further highlights the ability to use NR2F1 detection as a marker of target engagement and the power of using NR2F1 agonists as a treatment strategy not only in an adjuvant setting (M0 patients in the clinic) but also to cotarget proliferative and dormant DTCs coexisting in stage IV (M1) patients.</p>

2511 </div>

2512 <div id="8953023" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953020">

2513

2514 <p>Mechanistically, there are some open questions. For example, one specific NR2F1 antibody that was used in the Matrigel and CAM assays revealed a specific speckled nuclear localization of NR2F1 in large areas of the nucleus of the size of nucleoli. This signal was highly induced upon C26 treatment. Another NR2F1-specific antibody, which was used for paraffin-embedded lung tissues, reveals a more homogeneous specific nuclear signal. These differences may indicate that there are different nuclear NR2F1 pools (and epitopes) that might exhibit differential spatial localization and function, which requires further analysis. For example, it will be important to determine if C26 is inducing the same promoter and enhancer occupancy that NR2F1 shows during development (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib39" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Rada-Iglesias et al., 2012</a>) or in cells that have endogenously high NR2F1 levels. To this end our RNA-seq data and confirmatory experiments using quantitative RT-PCR revealed that signatures associated with neural crest differentiation and repression of EMT programs were regulated by C26, arguing that strong activation and up-regulation of NR2F1 via C26 may direct this nuclear receptor to activate these developmental programs while also suppressing mTOR signaling and proliferation programs, the latter commonly linked to a differentiated state. The inhibition of mTOR signaling strongly argues that C26 may also induce via NR2F1 a metabolic reprogramming that will be explored in separate studies. In HNSCC, mTOR inhibition has been tested as a potential therapy and showed mainly disease stabilization and partial response (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib37" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Patel et al., 2020</a>). It is tempting to speculate that agonists of NR2F1 plus mTOR inhibitors may be a potential effective treatment to manage residual but also overt recurrent disease in HNSCC. It was intriguing to find that patients with HNSCC primary tumors that expressed a C26-NR2F1 activity signature had better relapse-free and overall survival. This suggests that NR2F1-positive tumors may turn on a C26-like program that affects the fate of DTCs after dissemination. It also suggests that the signature, along with NR2F1 detection, may identify a priori patients who could benefit from an NR2F1 agonist. We have shown that primary tumor hypoxia can induce and NR2F1-dependent program that manifests after dissemination (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib17" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Fluegen et al., 2017</a>). However, it is unknown if the hypoxia-induced NR2F1 program overlaps with the C26 activity. We have not tested if C26 stabilizes the protein and whether this adds to the long-term effects we observed in vitro and in vivo. Finally, studies on reprogramming strategies, which were first designed in a cancer cell–centric way, revealed that they were also modulating the immune system to favor tumor control (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib30" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Lu et al., 2020</a>). We have so far not explored the contribution of non-T cell–dependent responses to the effect of C26 on metastasis in nude mouse PDX models.</p>

2515 </div>

2516 <div id="8953024" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953020">

2517

2518 <p>A long-term objective of our work is to provide proof-of-principle data that dormancy modulation can be exploited to find alternative strategies to prevent metastatic disease. Our experience using a combination of AZA and RA to activate global dormancy programs (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib44" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Sosa et al., 2015</a>) was a first step. This knowledge led to the development of an ongoing clinical trial that has as a goal to treat prostate cancer patients with biochemical relapse (NCT03572387). We have now advanced a step further by rationally designing a strategy based on the selective activation of NR2F1. Our data revealed that a selective NR2F1 agonist stably suppresses aggressive metastasis in a HNSCC PDX model, showing that it is possible to exploit dormancy mechanisms as therapeutic strategies to prevent metastasis. There is skepticism in the industry setting about advancing dormancy-inducing strategies, because there is a paralyzing misconception that trials need to last for many years, which would be costly (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib3" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Aguirre-Ghiso, 2021</a>). Our clinical trial in prostate cancer and other trials (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib3" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Aguirre-Ghiso, 2021</a>) are clear examples that it is not accurate to assume that readouts of dormancy therapies would take long to be obtained. One could envision many scenarios in which an agonist such as C26 could be tested within months. For example, in HNSCC, window-of-opportunity trials testing the biological response for further patient selection for treatment in the adjuvant setting could be designed by randomizing treatment between biopsy-based diagnosis and surgery. Overall, our study reveals a mechanism-based and rationally designed strategy to exploit NR2F1-activated dormancy as a therapeutic option to prevent metastatic relapse.</p>

2519 </div>

2520 </div>

2521 <h2 scrollto-destination="8953025" data-legacyid="s11" id="8953025" data-section-title="Materials and methods" class="section-title jumplink-heading">Materials and methods</h2>

2522 <div >

2523 </div>

2524 <h3 scrollto-destination="8953026" data-legacyid="s12" id="8953026" data-section-title="Cell lines" class="section-title ">Cell lines</h3>

2525 <div >

2526 <div id="8953027" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953026">

2527

2528 <p>T-HEp3 cells were derived from a lymph node metastasis from a HNSCC patient as described previously (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib34" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Ossowski and Reich, 1983</a>) and kept as PDXs on chick CAM. D-HEp3 cells were obtained by passing T-HEp3 cells for more than 40 generations in vitro (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib34" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Ossowski and Reich, 1983</a>). In vitro, these cells were cultured in DMEM with 10% of FBS and 100 U penicillin/0.1 mg/ml streptomycin. SQ20B, FaDu, and HEK293T cells were obtained from ATCC and grown in the same medium as HEp3 cells. All cells were grown at 5% CO<sub>2</sub> and 37°C and regularly tested for mycoplasma. Cell transfection was performed using Lipofectamine 3000 (Invitrogen) following manufacturer’s instructions. siRNA transfection was performed using Lipofectamine RNAiMAX (Invitrogen) following manufacturer’s instructions.</p>

2529 </div>

2530 </div>

2531 <h3 scrollto-destination="8953028" data-legacyid="s13" id="8953028" data-section-title="Reagents and antibodies" class="section-title ">Reagents and antibodies</h3>

2532 <div >

2533 <div id="8953029" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953028">

2534

2535 <p>C26 (molecular formula C<sub>28</sub>H<sub>30</sub>N<sub>4</sub>OS) was purchased from ChemBridge (6596020). <em>at</em>RA was purchased from Sigma-Aldrich (R2625). Human NR2F1 plasmid was a kind gift from Dr. Gilles Salbert (Rennes University, Rennes, France). Primary antibodies used for Western blot and immunofluorescence (IF) are listed in <a class="supplementary-material-link" reveal-id="all-supplement-materials-popup" data-open="all-supplement-materials-popup" path-from-xml="sup3">Table S3</a>. Human NR2F2 siRNA was purchased from Horizon Discovery (LQ-003422-00-0002).</p>

2536 </div>

2537 </div>

2538 <h3 scrollto-destination="8953030" data-legacyid="s14" id="8953030" data-section-title="In silico NR2F1 LBD modeling and agonist screen" class="section-title ">In silico NR2F1 LBD modeling and agonist screen</h3>

2539 <div >

2540 <div id="8953031" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953030">

2541

2542 <p>The 3D structure of NR2F1 LBD domain was predicted by comparative protein structure modeling based on its alignment with the structure of RXRα LBD, whose crystal structure in complex with 9-cis-RA has been solved (PDB accession no. <a class="link link-pdb" target="_blank" href="https://www.rcsb.org/structure/1FM6">1FM6</a>; <a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib19" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Gampe et al., 2000</a>). This analysis was performed using the software MODELLER v9.10 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib52" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Webb and Sali, 2014</a>). The resulting NR2F1 LBD was used to screen for potential agonists from a library of 110,000 drug-like small molecules (ChemBridge) using computational docking. Docking scores were generated using two different software packages, AutoDock 4 release 4.2.3 (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib20" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Goodsell et al., 1996</a>) and eHiTS (<a href="javascript:;" data-modal-source-id="bib55" class="link link-ref xref-bibr">Zsoldos et al., 2007</a>). In both cases, the docking grid was centered on the position of the 9-cis-RA in the RXRα structure. The target and the ligands were prepared using AutoDock Tools.</p>

2543 </div>

2544 </div>

2545 <h3 scrollto-destination="8953032" data-legacyid="s15" id="8953032" data-section-title="Luciferase reporter assay" class="section-title ">Luciferase reporter assay</h3>

2546 <div >

2547 <div id="8953033" class="article-section-wrapper js-article-section js-content-section " data-section-parent-id="8953032">

2548
